   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Eclipse
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Eclipse_(disambiguation)" title="Eclipse (disambiguation)">
        Eclipse (disambiguation)
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Total_Eclipse_(disambiguation)" title="Total Eclipse (disambiguation)">
        Total Eclipse (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3489" data-file-width="3543" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/250px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/375px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/500px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Totality during the 1999 solar eclipse.
         <a href="/wiki/Solar_prominence" title="Solar prominence">
          Solar prominences
         </a>
         can be seen along the limb (in red) as well as extensive
         <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">
          coronal
         </a>
         filaments.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       An
       <b>
        eclipse
       </b>
       is an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_event" title="Astronomical event">
        astronomical event
       </a>
       that occurs when an
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">
        astronomical object
       </a>
       is temporarily obscured, either by passing into the shadow of another body or by having another body pass between it and the viewer. This alignment of three celestial objects is known as a
       <a href="/wiki/Syzygy_(astronomy)" title="Syzygy (astronomy)">
        syzygy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Apart from syzygy, the term eclipse is also used when a spacecraft reaches a position where it can observe two celestial bodies so aligned. An eclipse is the result of either an
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">
        occultation
       </a>
       (completely hidden) or a
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_(astronomy)" title="Transit (astronomy)">
        transit
       </a>
       (partially hidden).
      </p>
      <p>
       The term eclipse is most often used to describe either a
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
        solar eclipse
       </a>
       , when the Moon's shadow crosses the Earth's surface, or a
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        lunar eclipse
       </a>
       , when the Moon moves into the Earth's shadow. However, it can also refer to such events beyond the Earth–Moon system: for example, a planet moving into the shadow cast by one of its moons, a moon passing into the shadow cast by its host planet, or a moon passing into the shadow of another moon. A
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">
        binary star
       </a>
       system can also produce eclipses if the plane of the orbit of its constituent stars intersects the observer's position.
      </p>
      <p>
       For the special cases of solar and lunar eclipses, these only happen during an "
       <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_season" title="Eclipse season">
        eclipse season
       </a>
       ", the two times of each year when the plane of the Earth's orbit around the Sun crosses with the plane of the Moon's orbit around the Earth. The
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse#Types" title="Solar eclipse">
        type of solar eclipse
       </a>
       that happens during each season (whether total, annular, hybrid, or partial) depends on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apparent_size" title="Apparent size">
        apparent sizes
       </a>
       of the Sun and Moon. If the orbit of the Earth around the Sun, and the Moon's orbit around the Earth were both in the same plane with each other, then eclipses would happen each and every month. There would be a lunar eclipse at every full moon, and a solar eclipse at every new moon. And if both orbits were perfectly circular, then each solar eclipse would be the same type every month. It is because of the non-planar and non-circular differences that eclipses are not a common event. Lunar eclipses can be viewed from the entire nightside half of the Earth. But solar eclipses, particularly a total eclipse, as occurring at any one particular point on the Earth's surface, is a rare event that can span many decades from one to the next.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">
        Etymology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The term is derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        ancient Greek
       </a>
       noun
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        ἔκλειψις
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="grc-Latn" xml:lang="grc-Latn">
         ékleipsis
        </span>
       </i>
       ), which means "the abandonment", "the downfall", or "the darkening of a heavenly body", which is derived from the verb
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        ἐκλείπω
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="grc-Latn" xml:lang="grc-Latn">
         ekleípō
        </span>
       </i>
       ) which means "to abandon", "to darken", or "to cease to exist,"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a combination of prefix
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        ἐκ-
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="grc-Latn" xml:lang="grc-Latn">
         ek-
        </span>
       </i>
       ), from preposition
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        ἐκ
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="grc-Latn" xml:lang="grc-Latn">
         ek
        </span>
       </i>
       ), "out," and of verb
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        λείπω
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="grc-Latn" xml:lang="grc-Latn">
         leípō
        </span>
       </i>
       ), "to be absent".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Umbra.2C_penumbra_and_antumbra">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Umbra,_penumbra_and_antumbra">
        Umbra, penumbra and antumbra
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Umbra, penumbra and antumbra">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .

      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Umbra,_penumbra_and_antumbra" title="Umbra, penumbra and antumbra">
        Umbra, penumbra and antumbra
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Umbra01.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="275" data-file-width="616" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Umbra01.svg/290px-Umbra01.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Umbra01.svg/435px-Umbra01.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Umbra01.svg/580px-Umbra01.svg.png 2x" width="290"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Umbra01.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Umbra, penumbra and antumbra cast by an opaque object occulting a larger light source
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       For any two objects in space, a line can be extended from the first through the second. The latter object will block some amount of light being emitted by the former, creating a region of shadow around the axis of the line. Typically these objects are moving with respect to each other and their surroundings, so the resulting shadow will sweep through a region of space, only passing through any particular location in the region for a fixed interval of time. As viewed from such a location, this shadowing event is known as an eclipse.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-westfall2014_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-westfall2014-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Typically the cross-section of the objects involved in an astronomical eclipse are roughly disk shaped.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-westfall2014_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-westfall2014-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The region of an object's shadow during an eclipse is divided into three parts:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-glossary-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umbra" title="Umbra">
          umbra
         </a>
        </i>
        , within which the object completely covers the light source. For the
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
        , this light source is the photosphere.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <i>
         antumbra
        </i>
        , extending beyond the tip of the umbra, within which the object is completely in front of the light source but too small to completely cover it.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <i>
         penumbra
        </i>
        , within which the object is only partially in front of the light source.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_types.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="296" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Solar_eclipse_types.svg/200px-Solar_eclipse_types.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Solar_eclipse_types.svg/300px-Solar_eclipse_types.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Solar_eclipse_types.svg/400px-Solar_eclipse_types.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_types.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sun-moon configurations that produce a total (A), annular (B), and partial (C) solar eclipse
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <b>
        total eclipse
       </b>
       occurs when the observer is within the umbra, an annular eclipse when the observer is within the antumbra, and a partial eclipse when the observer is within the penumbra. During a lunar eclipse only the umbra and penumbra are applicable. This is because Earth's apparent diameter from the viewpoint of the Moon is nearly four times that of the Sun. The same terms may be used analogously in describing other eclipses, e.g., the antumbra of Deimos crossing Mars, or Phobos entering Mars's penumbra.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        first contact
       </i>
       occurs when the eclipsing object's disc first starts to impinge on the light source;
       <i>
        second contact
       </i>
       is when the disc moves completely within the light source;
       <i>
        third contact
       </i>
       when it starts to move out of the light; and
       <i>
        fourth
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        last contact
       </i>
       when it finally leaves the light source's disc entirely.
      </p>
      <p>
       For spherical bodies, when the occulting object is smaller than the star, the length (
       <i>
        L
       </i>
       ) of the umbra's cone-shaped shadow is given by:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle L\ =\ {\frac {r\cdot R_{o}}{R_{s}-R_{o}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               L
              </mi>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mo>
                  ⋅
                  <!-- ⋅ -->
                 </mo>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   R
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi>
                    o
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   R
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi>
                    s
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <mo>
                  −
                  <!-- − -->
                 </mo>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   R
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi>
                    o
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle L\ =\ {\frac {r\cdot R_{o}}{R_{s}-R_{o}}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="L\ =\ \frac{r \cdot R_o}{R_s - R_o}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c3676ae17cac85ba635c6e4aec4bb5ced7f95c2" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:15.08ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        R
        <sub>
         s
        </sub>
       </i>
       is the radius of the star,
       <i>
        R
        <sub>
         o
        </sub>
       </i>
       is the occulting object's radius, and
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       is the distance from the star to the occulting object. For
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       , on average
       <i>
        L
       </i>
       is equal to 1.384
       <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
        ×
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         6
        </span>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometer" title="Kilometer">
        km
       </a>
       , which is much larger than the Moon's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semimajor_axis" title="Semimajor axis">
        semimajor axis
       </a>
       of 3.844
       <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
        ×
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         5
        </span>
       </sup>
       km. Hence the umbral cone of the Earth can completely envelop the Moon during a
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        lunar eclipse
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If the occulting object has an atmosphere, however, some of the luminosity of the star can be
       <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">
        refracted
       </a>
       into the volume of the umbra. This occurs, for example, during an eclipse of the Moon by the Earth—producing a faint,
       <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">
        ruddy
       </a>
       illumination of the Moon even at totality.
      </p>
      <p>
       On Earth, the shadow cast during an eclipse moves very approximately at 1 km per sec. This depends on the location of the shadow on the Earth and the angle in which it is moving.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eclipse_cycles">
        Eclipse cycles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Eclipse cycles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_cycle" title="Eclipse cycle">
        Eclipse cycle
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       An
       <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_cycle" title="Eclipse cycle">
        eclipse cycle
       </a>
       takes place when eclipses in a series are separated by a certain interval of time. This happens when the orbital motions of the bodies form repeating harmonic patterns. A particular instance is the
       <a href="/wiki/Saros_(astronomy)" title="Saros (astronomy)">
        saros
       </a>
       , which results in a repetition of a solar or lunar eclipse every 6,585.3 days, or a little over 18 years. Because this is not a whole number of days, successive eclipses will be visible from different parts of the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Earth.E2.80.93Moon_system">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Earth–Moon_system">
        Earth–Moon system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Earth–Moon system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="815" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg/240px-Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg/360px-Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg/480px-Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg.png 2x" width="240"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_eclipse_diagram-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A symbolic orbital diagram from the view of the Earth at the center, with the sun and moon projected upon the
         <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">
          celestial sphere
         </a>
         , showing the Moon's two nodes where eclipses can occur.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       An eclipse involving the Sun, Earth, and Moon can occur only when they are nearly in a straight line, allowing one to be hidden behind another, viewed from the third. Because the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_the_Moon" title="Orbit of the Moon">
        orbital plane of the Moon
       </a>
       is tilted with respect to the orbital plane of the Earth (the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
        ecliptic
       </a>
       ), eclipses can occur only when the Moon is close to the intersection of these two planes (the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Node_(astronomy)" title="Node (astronomy)">
        nodes
       </a>
       ). The Sun, Earth and nodes are aligned twice a year (during an
       <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_season" title="Eclipse season">
        eclipse season
       </a>
       ), and eclipses can occur during a period of about two months around these times. There can be from four to seven eclipses in a calendar year, which repeat according to various
       <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_cycle" title="Eclipse cycle">
        eclipse cycles
       </a>
       , such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Saros_(astronomy)" title="Saros (astronomy)">
        saros
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 1901 and 2100 there are the maximum of seven eclipses in:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        four (penumbral) lunar and three solar eclipses: 1908,
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_December_26,_2038#Related_eclipses" title="Solar eclipse of December 26, 2038">
         2038
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        four solar and three lunar eclipses: 1918,
        <a href="/wiki/December_1982_lunar_eclipse#Related_eclipses" title="December 1982 lunar eclipse">
         1973
        </a>
        , 2094.
       </li>
       <li>
        five solar and two lunar eclipses: 1934.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Excluding penumbral lunar eclipses, there are a maximum of seven eclipses in:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        1591, 1656, 1787, 1805, 1918, 1935, 1982, and 2094.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_eclipse">
        Solar eclipse
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Solar eclipse">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
        Solar eclipse
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2904" data-file-width="5104" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg/220px-2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg/330px-2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg/440px-2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2008-08-01_Solar_eclipse_progression_with_timestamps.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The progression of a
         <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
          solar eclipse
         </a>
         on August 1, 2008, viewed from
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novosibirsk,_Russia" title="Novosibirsk, Russia">
          Novosibirsk, Russia
         </a>
         . The time between shots is three minutes.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       As observed from the Earth, a
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
        solar eclipse
       </a>
       occurs when the Moon passes in front of the Sun. The type of solar eclipse event depends on the distance of the Moon from the Earth during the event. A total solar eclipse occurs when the Earth intersects the umbra portion of the Moon's shadow. When the umbra does not reach the surface of the Earth, the Sun is only partially occulted, resulting in an annular eclipse. Partial solar eclipses occur when the viewer is inside the penumbra.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg/170px-Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg/255px-Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg/340px-Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_visualisation.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Each icon shows the view from the centre of its black spot, representing the moon (not to scale)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_of_eclipse" title="Magnitude of eclipse">
        eclipse magnitude
       </a>
       is the fraction of the Sun's diameter that is covered by the Moon. For a total eclipse, this value is always greater than or equal to one. In both annular and total eclipses, the eclipse magnitude is the ratio of the angular sizes of the Moon to the Sun.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Solar eclipses are relatively brief events that can only be viewed in totality along a relatively narrow track. Under the most favorable circumstances, a total solar eclipse can last for 7 minutes, 31 seconds, and can be viewed along a track that is up to 250 km wide. However, the region where a partial eclipse can be observed is much larger. The Moon's umbra will advance eastward at a rate of 1,700 km/h, until it no longer intersects the Earth's surface.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="895" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg/300px-Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg/450px-Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg/600px-Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geometry_of_a_Total_Solar_Eclipse.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Geometry of a total solar eclipse (not to scale)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During a solar eclipse, the Moon can sometimes perfectly cover the Sun because its apparent size is nearly the same as the Sun's when viewed from the Earth. A total solar eclipse is in fact an
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">
        occultation
       </a>
       while an annular solar eclipse is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">
        transit
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       When observed at points in space other than from the Earth's surface, the Sun can be eclipsed by bodies other than the Moon. Two examples include when the crew of
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">
        Apollo 12
       </a>
       observed the
       <a href="/wiki/File:Earth_Eclipses_Sun-ap12-s80-37406.jpg" title="File:Earth Eclipses Sun-ap12-s80-37406.jpg">
        Earth to eclipse the Sun
       </a>
       in 1969 and when the
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
        Cassini
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">
        probe
       </a>
       observed
       <a href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg" title="File:Saturn eclipse.jpg">
        Saturn to eclipse the Sun
       </a>
       in 2006.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_eclipse">
        Lunar eclipse
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lunar eclipse">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        Lunar eclipse
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eclipse_lune.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="112" data-file-width="530" height="53" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eclipse_lune.jpg/250px-Eclipse_lune.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eclipse_lune.jpg/375px-Eclipse_lune.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eclipse_lune.jpg/500px-Eclipse_lune.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eclipse_lune.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The progression of a
         <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
          lunar eclipse
         </a>
         from right to left. Totality is shown with the first two images. These required a longer
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exposure_time" title="Exposure time">
          exposure time
         </a>
         to make the details visible.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Lunar eclipses occur when the Moon passes through the Earth's shadow. This happens only during a
       <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">
        full moon
       </a>
       , when the Moon is on the far side of the Earth from the Sun. Unlike a solar eclipse, an eclipse of the Moon can be observed from nearly an entire hemisphere. For this reason it is much more common to observe a lunar eclipse from a given location. A lunar eclipse lasts longer, taking several hours to complete, with totality itself usually averaging anywhere from about 30 minutes to over an hour.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are three types of lunar eclipses: penumbral, when the Moon crosses only the Earth's penumbra; partial, when the Moon crosses partially into the Earth's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umbra" title="Umbra">
        umbra
       </a>
       ; and total, when the Moon crosses entirely into the Earth's umbra. Total lunar eclipses pass through all three phases. Even during a total lunar eclipse, however, the Moon is not completely dark. Sunlight refracted through the Earth's atmosphere enters the umbra and provides a faint illumination. Much as in a sunset, the atmosphere tends to more strongly scatter light with shorter wavelengths, so the illumination of the Moon by refracted light has a red hue,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       thus the phrase 'Blood Moon' is often found in descriptions of such lunar events as far back as eclipses are recorded.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_record">
        Historical record
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Historical record">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Records of solar eclipses have been kept since ancient times. Eclipse dates can be used for
       <a href="/wiki/Chronological_dating" title="Chronological dating">
        chronological dating
       </a>
       of historical records. A
       <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
        Syrian
       </a>
       clay tablet, in the Ugaritic language, records a solar eclipse which occurred on March 5, 1223 B.C.,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while Paul Griffin argues that a stone in Ireland records an eclipse on November 30, 3340 B.C.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Positing classical-era astronomers' use of Babylonian eclipse records mostly from the 13th century BC provides a feasible and mathematically consistent
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       explanation for the Greek finding all three lunar mean motions (synodic, anomalistic, draconitic) to a precision of about one part in a million or better. Chinese historical records of solar eclipses date back over 4,000 years and have been used to measure changes in the Earth's rate of spin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the 1600s, European astronomers were publishing books with diagrams explaining how lunar and solar eclipses occurred.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In order to disseminate this information to a broader audience and decrease fear of the consequences of eclipses, booksellers printed broadsides explaining the event either using the science or via astrology.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_planets_and_dwarf_planets">
        Other planets and dwarf planets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other planets and dwarf planets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_giants">
        Gas giants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gas giants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Jupiter" title="Solar eclipses on Jupiter">
        Solar eclipses on Jupiter
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Saturn" title="Solar eclipses on Saturn">
        Solar eclipses on Saturn
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Uranus" title="Solar eclipses on Uranus">
        Solar eclipses on Uranus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Neptune" title="Solar eclipses on Neptune">
        Solar eclipses on Neptune
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JupiterandIo.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="720" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/JupiterandIo.jpg/220px-JupiterandIo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/JupiterandIo.jpg/330px-JupiterandIo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/JupiterandIo.jpg/440px-JupiterandIo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JupiterandIo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A picture of
         <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
          Jupiter
         </a>
         and its moon
         <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
          Io
         </a>
         taken by
         <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
          Hubble
         </a>
         . The black spot is Io's shadow.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1364" data-file-width="2766" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/250px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/375px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/500px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
          Saturn
         </a>
         occults the Sun as seen from the
         <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
          Cassini–Huygens
         </a>
         space probe
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">
        gas giant
       </a>
       planets (
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CallistoSun_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CallistoSun-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TitanSun_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TitanSun-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
        Uranus
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MirandaSun_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MirandaSun-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
        Neptune
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NereidSun_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NereidSun-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have many moons and thus frequently display eclipses. The most striking involve
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       , which has four large moons and a low
       <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">
        axial tilt
       </a>
       , making eclipses more frequent as these bodies pass through the shadow of the larger planet. Transits occur with equal frequency. It is common to see the larger moons casting circular shadows upon Jupiter's cloudtops.
      </p>
      <p>
       The eclipses of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       by Jupiter became accurately predictable once their orbital elements were known. During the 1670s, it was discovered that these events were occurring about 17 minutes later than expected when Jupiter was on the far side of the Sun.
       <a href="/wiki/Ole_R%C3%B8mer" title="Ole Rømer">
        Ole Rømer
       </a>
       deduced that the delay was caused by the time needed for light to travel from Jupiter to the Earth. This was used to produce the first estimate of the
       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">
        speed of light
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On the other three
       <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">
        gas giants
       </a>
       , eclipses only occur at certain periods during the planet's orbit, due to their higher
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">
        inclination
       </a>
       between the orbits of the moon and the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">
        orbital plane
       </a>
       of the planet. The moon
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       , for example, has an orbital plane tilted about 1.6° to Saturn's equatorial plane. But Saturn has an axial tilt of nearly 27°. The orbital plane of Titan only crosses the line of sight to the Sun at two points along Saturn's orbit. As the orbital period of Saturn is 29.7 years, an eclipse is only possible about every 15 years.
      </p>
      <p>
       The timing of the Jovian satellite eclipses was also used to calculate an observer's
       <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
        longitude
       </a>
       upon the Earth. By knowing the expected time when an eclipse would be observed at a standard longitude (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich" title="Greenwich">
        Greenwich
       </a>
       ), the time difference could be computed by accurately observing the local time of the eclipse. The time difference gives the longitude of the observer because every hour of difference corresponded to 15° around the Earth's equator. This technique was used, for example, by
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">
        Giovanni D. Cassini
       </a>
       in 1679 to re-map
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">
        Mars
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mars">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Phobos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Phobos from Mars">
        Transit of Phobos from Mars
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA05553.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="279" data-file-width="291" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PIA05553.gif/220px-PIA05553.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/PIA05553.gif 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA05553.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Phobos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Phobos from Mars">
          Transit of Phobos from Mars
         </a>
         , as seen by the Mars
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_(rover)" title="Opportunity (rover)">
           Opportunity rover
          </a>
         </i>
         (10 March 2004).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       , only partial solar eclipses (
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Phobos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Phobos from Mars">
        transits
       </a>
       ) are possible, because neither of its moons is large enough, at their respective orbital radii, to cover the Sun's disc as seen from the surface of the planet. Eclipses of the moons by Mars are not only possible, but commonplace, with hundreds occurring each Earth year. There are also rare occasions when Deimos is eclipsed by Phobos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Martian eclipses have been photographed from both the surface of Mars and from orbit.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pluto">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Pluto" title="Solar eclipses on Pluto">
        Solar eclipses on Pluto
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       , with its proportionately largest moon
       <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
        Charon
       </a>
       , is also the site of many eclipses. A series of such mutual eclipses occurred between 1985 and 1990.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These daily events led to the first accurate measurements of the physical parameters of both objects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_and_Venus">
        Mercury and Venus
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mercury and Venus">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Eclipses are impossible on
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       , which have no moons. However, both have been observed to transit across the face of the Sun. There are on average 13 transits of Mercury each century. Transits of Venus occur in pairs separated by an interval of eight years, but each pair of events happen less than once a century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to NASA, the next pair of transits will occur on December 10, 2117 and December 8, 2125. Transits on Mercury are much more common.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eclipsing_binaries">
        Eclipsing binaries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eclipse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Eclipsing binaries">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">
        binary star
       </a>
       system consists of two stars that orbit around their common
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_of_mass" title="Centre of mass">
        centre of mass
       </a>
       . The movements of both stars lie on a common
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">
        orbital plane
       </a>
       in space. When this plane is very closely aligned with the location of an observer, the stars can be seen to pass in front of each other. The result is a type of extrinsic
       <a href="/wiki/Variable_star" title="Variable star">
        variable star
       </a>
       system called an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclipsing_binary" title="Eclipsing binary">
        eclipsing binary
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The maximum
       <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">
        luminosity
       </a>
       of an eclipsing binary system is equal to the sum of the luminosity contributions from the individual stars. When one star passes in front of the other, the luminosity of the system is seen to decrease. The luminosity returns to normal once the two stars are no longer in alignment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first eclipsing binary star system to be discovered was
       <a href="/wiki/Algol" title="Algol">
        Algol
       </a>
       , a star system in the constellation
       <a href="/wiki/Perseus_(constellation)" title="Perseus (constellation)">
        Perseus
       </a>
       . Normally this star system has a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_magnitude" title="Visual magnitude">
        visual magnitude
       </a>
       of 2.1. However, every 2.867 days the magnitude decreases to 3.4 for more than nine hours. This is caused by the passage of the dimmer member of the pair in front of the brighter star.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The concept that an eclipsing body caused these luminosity variations was introduced by
       <a href="/wiki/John_Goodricke" title="John Goodricke">
        John Goodricke
       </a>
       in 1783.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft.atitle=The+earliest+known+solar+eclipse+record+redated&amp;rft.volume=338&amp;rft.issue=6212&amp;rft.pages=238-240&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2F338238a0&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1989Natur.338..238D&amp;rft.aulast=de+Jong&amp;rft.aufirst=T.&amp;rft.au=van+Soldt%2C+W.+H.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nature.com%2Fnature%2Fjournal%2Fv338%2Fn6212%2Fabs%2F338238a0.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEclipse">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>

         <li id="cite_note-18">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-18">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation web">
            Griffin, Paul (2002).
            <a class="external text" href="http://www.astronomy.ca/3340eclipse/" rel="nofollow">
             "Confirmation of World's Oldest Solar Eclipse Recorded in Stone"
            </a>
            . The Digital Universe
            <span class="reference-accessdate">
             . Retrieved
             <span class="nowrap">
              2007-05-02
             </span>
            </span>
            .
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Confirmation+of+World%27s+Oldest+Solar+Eclipse+Recorded+in+Stone&amp;rft.pub=The+Digital+Universe&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Griffin&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.astronomy.ca%2F3340eclipse%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEclipse">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-19">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-19">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           See
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/vols/wg0.pdf" rel="nofollow">
            DIO 16
           </a>
           p.2 (2009). Though those Greek and perhaps Babylonian astronomers who determined the three previously unsolved lunar motions were spread over more than four centuries (263 BC to 160 AD), the math-indicated early eclipse records are all from a
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/thr.htm#ufnr" rel="nofollow">
            much smaller span
           </a>
           : the 13th century BC. The anciently attested Greek technique: use of eclipse cycles, automatically providing integral ratios, which is how all ancient astronomers' lunar motions were expressed. Long-eclipse-cycle-based reconstructions precisely produce all of the 24 digits appearing in the three attested ancient motions just cited: 6247 synod = 6695 anom (System A), 5458 synod = 5923 drac (Hipparchos), 3277 synod = 3512 anom (Planetary Hypotheses). By contrast, the System B motion, 251 synod = 269 anom (Aristarchos?), could have been determined without recourse to remote eclipse data, simply by using a few eclipse-pairs 4267 months apart.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-20">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-20">
             ^
            </a>

          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation web">
            <a class="external text" href="http://www.bibalex.org/eclipse2006/HistoricalObservationsofSolarEclipses.htm" rel="nofollow">
             "Solar Eclipses in History and Mythology"
            </a>
            . Bibliotheca Alexandrina
            <span class="reference-accessdate">
             . Retrieved
             <span class="nowrap">
              2007-05-02
             </span>
            </span>
            .
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Solar+Eclipses+in+History+and+Mythology&amp;rft.pub=Bibliotheca+Alexandrina&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bibalex.org%2Feclipse2006%2FHistoricalObservationsofSolarEclipses.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEclipse">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-21">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-21">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation book">
            Girault, Simon (1592).
            <i>
             Globe dv monde contenant un bref traite du ciel &amp; de la terra
            </i>
